### [1] [SAC-NeRF: Adaptive Ray Sampling for Neural Radiance Fields via Soft Actor-Critic Reinforcement Learning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.15622)
*Chenyu Ge*

Main category: cs.CV

TL;DR: SAC-NeRF uses a Soft Actor-Critic agent to adaptively place NeRF ray samples, cutting 35–48% of samples with only 0.3–0.8 dB PSNR loss versus dense sampling.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: NeRF rendering is slow because it relies on dense, fixed sampling along rays. Hand-crafted heuristics struggle to balance quality and efficiency across diverse scenes. The paper aims to learn data-driven sampling that spends compute where it matters while preserving image fidelity.

Method: Formulate per-ray sampling as an MDP and train a policy with Soft Actor-Critic. Three key pieces: (1) a Gaussian mixture color model to estimate predictive uncertainty that guides decisions; (2) a multi-term reward that balances image quality, sampling efficiency, and temporal/scene consistency; (3) a two-stage training regime to mitigate non-stationarity as both NeRF and policy evolve.

Result: On Synthetic-NeRF and LLFF, the learned policy reduces sampling by 35–48% while keeping PSNR within 0.3–0.8 dB of dense baselines. Policies are scene-specific and training adds overhead compared to simple heuristics.

Conclusion: Adaptive, RL-driven sampling can substantially reduce NeRF compute with minimal quality loss, though it introduces complexity and limited cross-scene generalization. It shows that learned strategies can surpass hand-designed sampling rules.

Abstract: Neural Radiance Fields (NeRF) have achieved photorealistic novel view synthesis but suffer from computational inefficiency due to dense ray sampling during volume rendering. We propose SAC-NeRF, a reinforcement learning framework that learns adaptive sampling policies using Soft Actor-Critic (SAC). Our method formulates sampling as a Markov Decision Process where an RL agent learns to allocate samples based on scene characteristics. We introduce three technical components: (1) a Gaussian mixture distribution color model providing uncertainty estimates, (2) a multi-component reward function balancing quality, efficiency, and consistency, and (3) a two-stage training strategy addressing environment non-stationarity. Experiments on Synthetic-NeRF and LLFF datasets show that SAC-NeRF reduces sampling points by 35-48\% while maintaining rendering quality within 0.3-0.8 dB PSNR of dense sampling baselines. While the learned policy is scene-specific and the RL framework adds complexity compared to simpler heuristics, our work demonstrates that data-driven sampling strategies can discover effective patterns that would be difficult to hand-design.

</details>


### [2] [Exploring the Use of VLMs for Navigation Assistance for People with Blindness and Low Vision](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.15624)
*Yu Li,Yuchen Zheng,Giles Hamilton-Fletcher,Marco Mezzavilla,Yao Wang,Sundeep Rangan,Maurizio Porfiri,Zhou Yu,John-Ross Rizzo*

Main category: cs.CV

TL;DR: Benchmarks multiple state-of-the-art vision–language models for assisting blind/low-vision navigation, finding GPT-4o strongest while open-source models lag, with shared weaknesses in counting, spatial reasoning, and prioritizing object details over spatial feedback; VLMs show promise with better alignment and spatial skills.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Safe, reliable wayfinding is a critical unmet need for people who are blind or have low vision. Existing VLMs can describe scenes but their suitability for navigation-centric perception and reasoning is unclear. A systematic comparison can highlight capability gaps and guide assistive tech integration.

Method: Evaluate closed-source (GPT-4V/4o, Gemini-1.5-Pro, Claude-3.5-Sonnet) and open-source (LLaVA variants) VLMs on foundational skills—obstacle counting, relative spatial reasoning, and commonsense, wayfinding-relevant scene understanding—plus pBLV-specific navigation prompts that simulate real assistance tasks.

Result: Clear performance disparities: GPT-4o consistently leads, especially in spatial reasoning and scene understanding. Open-source models underperform on nuanced reasoning and adaptability. Common failure modes include miscounting in clutter, spatial biases, and overemphasis on object attributes rather than actionable spatial guidance.

Conclusion: Current VLMs are not yet reliable for independent navigation but hold promise if aligned with human feedback and augmented with stronger spatial reasoning. The study offers practical guidance for integrating VLMs into assistive systems while targeting key weaknesses to improve usability.

Abstract: This paper investigates the potential of vision-language models (VLMs) to assist people with blindness and low vision (pBLV) in navigation tasks. We evaluate state-of-the-art closed-source models, including GPT-4V, GPT-4o, Gemini-1.5-Pro, and Claude-3.5-Sonnet, alongside open-source models, such as Llava-v1.6-mistral and Llava-onevision-qwen, to analyze their capabilities in foundational visual skills: counting ambient obstacles, relative spatial reasoning, and common-sense wayfinding-pertinent scene understanding. We further assess their performance in navigation scenarios, using pBLV-specific prompts designed to simulate real-world assistance tasks. Our findings reveal notable performance disparities between these models: GPT-4o consistently outperforms others across all tasks, particularly in spatial reasoning and scene understanding. In contrast, open-source models struggle with nuanced reasoning and adaptability in complex environments. Common challenges include difficulties in accurately counting objects in cluttered settings, biases in spatial reasoning, and a tendency to prioritize object details over spatial feedback, limiting their usability for pBLV in navigation tasks. Despite these limitations, VLMs show promise for wayfinding assistance when better aligned with human feedback and equipped with improved spatial reasoning. This research provides actionable insights into the strengths and limitations of current VLMs, guiding developers on effectively integrating VLMs into assistive technologies while addressing key limitations for enhanced usability.

</details>


### [3] [Improving Generative Adversarial Network Generalization for Facial Expression Synthesis](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.15648)
*Arbish Akram,Nazar Khan,Arif Mahmood*

Main category: cs.CV

TL;DR: RegGAN combines a locally regularized regression stage with adversarial refinement to learn an intermediate expression representation that generalizes beyond the training set, delivering SOTA realism and expression quality with near-SOTA identity preservation.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Conditional GANs for facial expression synthesis often overfit and degrade on out-of-distribution inputs; there is a need to generate realistic expressions while preserving identity across diverse domains (e.g., celebrities, portraits, statues, avatars).

Method: Two-stage architecture: (1) a regression layer with local receptive fields trained via ridge regression to reconstruct expression details, producing an intermediate representation; (2) a refinement network trained adversarially to enhance realism. Trained on CFEE; evaluated in-distribution and OOD using ECS (expression), FSS (identity), QualiCLIP (perceptual realism), and FID (quality/realism).

Result: Across six SOTA baselines, RegGAN achieves superior ECS, FID, and QualiCLIP, and ranks second on FSS. Human studies report +25% expression quality, +26% identity preservation, and +30% realism over the best competing model.

Conclusion: A locally constrained regression-based intermediate representation, followed by adversarial refinement, improves generalization and perceptual quality for facial expression synthesis, with only a slight shortfall on identity preservation relative to the top method.

Abstract: Facial expression synthesis aims to generate realistic facial expressions while preserving identity. Existing conditional generative adversarial networks (GANs) achieve excellent image-to-image translation results, but their performance often degrades when test images differ from the training dataset. We present Regression GAN (RegGAN), a model that learns an intermediate representation to improve generalization beyond the training distribution. RegGAN consists of two components: a regression layer with local receptive fields that learns expression details by minimizing the reconstruction error through a ridge regression loss, and a refinement network trained adversarially to enhance the realism of generated images. We train RegGAN on the CFEE dataset and evaluate its generalization performance both on CFEE and challenging out-of-distribution images, including celebrity photos, portraits, statues, and avatar renderings. For evaluation, we employ four widely used metrics: Expression Classification Score (ECS) for expression quality, Face Similarity Score (FSS) for identity preservation, QualiCLIP for perceptual realism, and Fréchet Inception Distance (FID) for assessing both expression quality and realism. RegGAN outperforms six state-of-the-art models in ECS, FID, and QualiCLIP, while ranking second in FSS. Human evaluations indicate that RegGAN surpasses the best competing model by 25% in expression quality, 26% in identity preservation, and 30% in realism.

</details>


### [4] [OrthoAI v2: From Single-Agent Segmentation to Dual-Agent Treatment Planning for Clear Aligners](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.15663)
*Lansiaux Edouard,Leman Margaux*

Main category: cs.CV

TL;DR: OrthoAI v2 is a dual‑agent, landmark‑aware orthodontic planning pipeline that adds a composite biomechanical scoring system and a 4D staging simulator, achieving a ~21% relative gain in planning quality over v1 while remaining CPU‑deployable.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: v1 could only extract tooth centroids from segmentation, lacked landmark‑level precision, provided a single scalar pass/fail score, and had no treatment staging simulation—insufficient for clinically realistic, explainable, and actionable clear‑aligner planning.

Method: Introduce a second agent using conditioned heatmap regression (CHARM) for direct, segmentation‑free dental landmark detection and fuse it with the v1 DGCNN segmentation agent via a confidence‑weighted orchestrator (parallel, sequential, or single‑agent modes). Replace the binary check with a six‑category weighted score (biomechanics, staging, attachments, IPR, occlusion, predictability). Add a multi‑frame treatment simulator that generates temporally coherent 6‑DoF tooth trajectories using SLERP and evidence‑based staging rules (F = A × r), enabling ClinCheck‑style 4D visualization.

Result: On a synthetic benchmark of 200 crowding cases, the parallel ensemble of v2 achieves a planning quality score of 92.8 ± 4.1 vs 76.4 ± 8.3 for v1 (+21% relative gain) with full CPU deployability (4.2 ± 0.8 s).

Conclusion: OrthoAI v2 overcomes v1’s precision and evaluation gaps by fusing segmentation and landmarking, introducing a clinically relevant multi‑factor scoring model, and simulating staged 4D movements, yielding higher‑quality plans without sacrificing deployability.

Abstract: We present OrthoAI v2, the second iteration of our open-source pipeline for AI-assisted orthodontic treatment planning with clear aligners, substantially extending the single-agent framework previously introduced. The first version established a proof-of-concept based on Dynamic Graph Convolutional Neural Networks (\dgcnn{}) for tooth segmentation but was limited to per-tooth centroid extraction, lacked landmark-level precision, and produced a scalar quality score without staging simulation. \vtwo{} addresses all three limitations through three principal contributions: (i)~a second agent adopting the Conditioned Heatmap Regression Methodology (\charm{})~\cite{rodriguez2025charm} for direct, segmentation-free dental landmark detection, fused with Agent~1 via a confidence-weighted orchestrator in three modes (parallel, sequential, single-agent); (ii)~a composite six-category biomechanical scoring model (biomechanics $\times$ 0.30 + staging $\times$ 0.20 + attachments $\times$ 0.15 + IPR $\times$ 0.10 + occlusion $\times$ 0.10 + predictability $\times$ 0.15) replacing the binary pass/fail check of v1; (iii)~a multi-frame treatment simulator generating $F = A \times r$ temporally coherent 6-DoF tooth trajectories via SLERP interpolation and evidence-based staging rules, enabling ClinCheck 4D visualisation. On a synthetic benchmark of 200 crowding scenarios, the parallel ensemble of OrthoAI v2 reaches a planning quality score of $92.8 \pm 4.1$ vs.\ $76.4 \pm 8.3$ for OrthoAI v1, a $+21\%$ relative gain, while maintaining full CPU deployability ($4.2 \pm 0.8$~s).

</details>


### [5] [CLRNet: Targetless Extrinsic Calibration for Camera, Lidar and 4D Radar Using Deep Learning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.15767)
*Marcell Kegl,Andras Palffy,Csaba Benedek,Dariu M. Gavrila*

Main category: cs.CV

TL;DR: CLRNet is an end-to-end deep learning method for joint or pairwise extrinsic calibration among camera, lidar, and 4D radar, leveraging equirectangular projection, camera-predicted depth, enriched radar channels, a shared cross-modal feature space with lidar, and a loop-closure loss. It achieves ≥50% reductions in median translation and rotation errors over prior methods on View-of-Delft and Dual-Radar, and examines cross-dataset transfer.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Extrinsic calibration with radar is notoriously difficult due to sparse, noisy measurements. A unified, accurate, and preferably automated calibration approach for camera–lidar–radar stacks is needed for robust multi-sensor perception in autonomous systems.

Method: Introduce CLRNet, a multi-modal end-to-end calibration network handling joint or pairwise calibration across camera, lidar, and 4D radar. Key components: equirectangular projection to harmonize sensor geometries; depth prediction from the camera stream; additional radar input channels (e.g., Doppler/RCS); a shared feature space with lidar to facilitate cross-sensor alignment; and a loop-closure loss to enforce geometric consistency.

Result: On View-of-Delft and Dual-Radar, CLRNet outperforms state-of-the-art baselines, cutting median translational and rotational calibration errors by at least 50%. Authors also assess domain transfer by training and evaluating across datasets.

Conclusion: CLRNet provides a general, accurate DL-based solution for calibrating camera–lidar–radar systems, with strong gains over existing methods and some demonstrated cross-dataset robustness, suggesting practical applicability for autonomous sensing platforms.

Abstract: In this paper, we address extrinsic calibration for camera, lidar, and 4D radar sensors. Accurate extrinsic calibration of radar remains a challenge due to the sparsity of its data. We propose CLRNet, a novel, multi-modal end-to-end deep learning (DL) calibration network capable of addressing joint camera-lidar-radar calibration, or pairwise calibration between any two of these sensors. We incorporate equirectangular projection, camera-based depth image prediction, additional radar channels, and leverage lidar with a shared feature space and loop closure loss. In extensive experiments using the View-of-Delft and Dual-Radar datasets, we demonstrate superior calibration accuracy compared to existing state-of-the-art methods, reducing both median translational and rotational calibration errors by at least 50%. Finally, we examine the domain transfer capabilities of the proposed network and baselines, when evaluating across datasets. The code will be made publicly available upon acceptance at: https://github.com/tudelft-iv.

</details>


### [6] [Domain Adaptation Without the Compute Burden for Efficient Whole Slide Image Analysis](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.15774)
*Umar Marikkar,Muhammad Awais,Sara Atito*

Main category: cs.CV

TL;DR: eWSI integrates parameter‑efficient fine‑tuning with MIL to enable end‑to‑end, task‑specific WSI training that matches or beats in‑domain pretrained baselines using only ImageNet features, and often improves further when paired with in‑domain extractors—reducing the need for costly domain pretraining.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: WSIs are too large for conventional end‑to‑end training, so pipelines rely on fixed features from ImageNet backbones plus MIL. ImageNet features miss histopathology nuances, while in‑domain pretraining is expensive and still not task‑specific. A method is needed that is both computationally efficient and task‑targeted.

Method: EfficientWSI (eWSI) combines Parameter‑Efficient Fine‑Tuning (e.g., lightweight adapter/LoRA‑style updates) with Multiple Instance Learning to allow end‑to‑end optimization on WSI tasks while updating only a small subset of backbone parameters. Evaluated across seven slide‑level classification tasks on Camelyon16, TCGA, and BRACS.

Result: With ImageNet‑pretrained extractors, eWSI achieves strong classification, matching or outperforming MIL systems that use in‑domain pretrained features—mitigating the need for extensive domain‑specific pretraining. When paired with in‑domain extractors, eWSI further improves performance in most tasks, indicating effective capture of task‑specific information.

Conclusion: eWSI offers a computationally efficient, task‑targeted pathway for WSI analysis, enabling practical end‑to‑end training and advancing task‑specific learning in computational pathology while reducing reliance on costly in‑domain pretraining.

Abstract: Computational methods on analyzing Whole Slide Images (WSIs) enable early diagnosis and treatments by supporting pathologists in detection and classification of tumors. However, the extremely high resolution of WSIs makes end-to-end training impractical compared to typical image analysis tasks. To address this, most approaches use pre-trained feature extractors to obtain fixed representations of whole slides, which are then combined with Multiple Instance Learning (MIL) for downstream tasks. These feature extractors are typically pre-trained on natural image datasets such as ImageNet, which fail to capture domain-specific characteristics. Although domain-specific pre-training on histopathology data yields more relevant feature representations, it remains computationally expensive and fail to capture task-specific characteristics within the domain. To address the computational cost and lack of task-specificity in domain-specific pre-training, we propose EfficientWSI (eWSI), a careful integration of Parameter-Efficient-Fine-Tuning (PEFT) and Multiple Instance Learning (MIL) that enables end-to-end training on WSI tasks. We evaluate eWSI on seven WSI-level tasks over Camelyon16, TCGA and BRACS datasets. Our results show that eWSI when applied with ImageNet feature extractors yields strong classification performance, matching or outperforming MILs with in-domain feature extractors, alleviating the need for extensive in-domain pre-training. Furthermore, when eWSI is applied with in-domain feature extractors, it further improves classification performance in most cases, demonstrating its ability to capture task-specific information where beneficial. Our findings suggest that eWSI provides a task-targeted, computationally efficient path for WSI tasks, offering a promising direction for task-specific learning in computational pathology.

</details>


### [7] [Parallelised Differentiable Straightest Geodesics for 3D Meshes](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.15780)
*Hippolyte Verninas,Caner Korkmaz,Stefanos Zafeiriou,Tolga Birdal,Simone Foti*

Main category: cs.CV

TL;DR: Differentiable, GPU-parallel exponential map on triangle meshes via straightest geodesics, with two gradient routes (extrinsic proxy and geodesic finite differences), enabling geodesic layers, mesh flow matching, and second-order optimization; released as the digeo library.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Learning on curved surfaces/meshes lags behind Euclidean ML due to missing closed-form Riemannian operators, nondifferentiable discrete substitutes, and poor parallelization—blocking end-to-end training and scalable deployment.

Method: Use the straightest geodesics framework to compute the exponential map on mesh-discretized Riemannian surfaces; implement a parallel GPU pipeline and provide two differentiation strategies: (1) an extrinsic proxy objective; (2) a geodesic finite-difference scheme. The framework also supports geodesic tracing and parallel transport.

Result: They demonstrate accurate and highly parallel performance of the exponential map and show downstream gains by plugging it into ML/optimization tasks. They introduce: (i) a geodesic convolutional layer, (ii) a flow-matching method for meshes, and (iii) a second-order optimizer applied to centroidal Voronoi tessellation.

Conclusion: A practical, differentiable, and scalable geodesic/exponential-map computation on meshes unlocks Riemannian operations for learning and optimization on surfaces; the open-source digeo package provides ready-to-use implementations.

Abstract: Machine learning has been progressively generalised to operate within non-Euclidean domains, but geometrically accurate methods for learning on surfaces are still falling behind. The lack of closed-form Riemannian operators, the non-differentiability of their discrete counterparts, and poor parallelisation capabilities have been the main obstacles to the development of the field on meshes. A principled framework to compute the exponential map on Riemannian surfaces discretised as meshes is straightest geodesics, which also allows to trace geodesics and parallel-transport vectors as a by-product. We provide a parallel GPU implementation and derive two different methods for differentiating through the straightest geodesics, one leveraging an extrinsic proxy function and one based upon a geodesic finite differences scheme. After proving our parallelisation performance and accuracy, we demonstrate how our differentiable exponential map can improve learning and optimisation pipelines on general geometries. In particular, to showcase the versatility of our method, we propose a new geodesic convolutional layer, a new flow matching method for learning on meshes, and a second-order optimiser that we apply to centroidal Voronoi tessellation. Our code, models, and pip-installable library (digeo) are available at: circle-group.github.io/research/DSG.

</details>


### [8] [Evolving Contextual Safety in Multi-Modal Large Language Models via Inference-Time Self-Reflective Memory](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.15800)
*Ce Zhang,Jinxi He,Junyi He,Katia Sycara,Yaqi Xie*

Main category: cs.CV

TL;DR: Introduces a contextual safety benchmark (MM-SafetyBench++) with paired safe/unsafe image–text prompts and a training-free method (EchoSafe) that uses a self-reflective memory bank to retrieve prior safety insights during inference, improving MLLM safety decisions across benchmarks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing MLLM safety defenses largely detect explicit unsafe inputs but miss subtle, context-dependent intent changes. The field lacks a controlled way to test whether models can differentiate near-identical scenarios with opposite safety implications and mechanisms to adapt safety behavior based on prior experience.

Method: 1) Benchmark: For each unsafe image–text pair, minimally edit it to flip user intent while preserving contextual content, yielding matched safe/unsafe pairs for controlled evaluation of context sensitivity. 2) EchoSafe: A training-free, inference-time framework that maintains a self-reflective memory bank of prior interactions and safety rationales, retrieves relevant experiences, and injects them into current prompts to enable context-aware reasoning and continual adaptation.

Result: Across multiple multi-modal safety benchmarks, EchoSafe consistently outperforms baselines, establishing a strong baseline for contextual safety in MLLMs (specific metrics not provided in the abstract).

Conclusion: Contextual safety is a critical gap for MLLMs. MM-SafetyBench++ enables precise evaluation of intent sensitivity, and EchoSafe offers an effective, training-free path to improve context-aware safety at inference. Data and code are released for community use.

Abstract: Multi-modal Large Language Models (MLLMs) have achieved remarkable performance across a wide range of visual reasoning tasks, yet their vulnerability to safety risks remains a pressing concern. While prior research primarily focuses on jailbreak defenses that detect and refuse explicitly unsafe inputs, such approaches often overlook contextual safety, which requires models to distinguish subtle contextual differences between scenarios that may appear similar but diverge significantly in safety intent. In this work, we present MM-SafetyBench++, a carefully curated benchmark designed for contextual safety evaluation. Specifically, for each unsafe image-text pair, we construct a corresponding safe counterpart through minimal modifications that flip the user intent while preserving the underlying contextual meaning, enabling controlled evaluation of whether models can adapt their safety behaviors based on contextual understanding. Further, we introduce EchoSafe, a training-free framework that maintains a self-reflective memory bank to accumulate and retrieve safety insights from prior interactions. By integrating relevant past experiences into current prompts, EchoSafe enables context-aware reasoning and continual evolution of safety behavior during inference. Extensive experiments on various multi-modal safety benchmarks demonstrate that EchoSafe consistently achieves superior performance, establishing a strong baseline for advancing contextual safety in MLLMs. All benchmark data and code are available at https://echosafe-mllm.github.io.

</details>


### [9] [Feed-forward Gaussian Registration for Head Avatar Creation and Editing](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.15811)
*Malte Prinzler,Paulo Gotardo,Siyu Tang,Timo Bolkart*

Main category: cs.CV

TL;DR: MATCH introduces a fast, correspondence-aware multi-view Gaussian method that predicts UV-aligned Gaussian splat textures for high-quality, editable head avatars in 0.5s/frame, outperforming prior work while being ~10× faster.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing multi-view head avatar pipelines are slow and cumbersome, requiring separate head tracking and costly optimization that can take over a day. There is a need for a method that produces high-quality avatars quickly, with robust correspondences across frames and subjects to enable editing and transfer tasks.

Method: A transformer-based model directly predicts Gaussian splat textures in the fixed UV layout of a template mesh from calibrated multi-view images. It learns intra- and inter-subject correspondences end-to-end via a registration-guided attention block, where each UV token attends only to image tokens of its corresponding mesh region, improving efficiency and accuracy over dense cross-view attention. No preprocessing is required.

Result: MATCH generates avatars at 0.5 seconds per frame and outperforms state-of-the-art methods in novel-view synthesis, geometry registration, and head avatar generation. It enables applications such as expression transfer, optimization-free tracking, semantic editing, and identity interpolation. Avatar creation is ~10× faster than the closest baseline. Code and weights are released.

Conclusion: By aligning UV-space predictions with registration-guided attention, MATCH delivers rapid, high-quality, and semantically consistent head avatars, offering strong performance and broad editing capabilities with significantly reduced creation time.

Abstract: We present MATCH (Multi-view Avatars from Topologically Corresponding Heads), a multi-view Gaussian registration method for high-quality head avatar creation and editing. State-of-the-art multi-view head avatar methods require time-consuming head tracking followed by expensive avatar optimization, often resulting in a total creation time of more than one day. MATCH, in contrast, directly predicts Gaussian splat textures in correspondence from calibrated multi-view images in just 0.5 seconds per frame, without requiring data preprocessing. The learned intra-subject correspondence across frames enables fast creation of personalized head avatars, while correspondence across subjects supports applications such as expression transfer, optimization-free tracking, semantic editing, and identity interpolation. We establish these correspondences end-to-end using a transformer-based model that predicts Gaussian splat textures in the fixed UV layout of a template mesh. To achieve this, we introduce a novel registration-guided attention block, where each UV-map token attends exclusively to image tokens depicting its corresponding mesh region. This design improves efficiency and performance compared to dense cross-view attention. MATCH outperforms existing methods in novel-view synthesis, geometry registration, and head avatar generation, while making avatar creation 10 times faster than the closest competing baseline. The code and model weights are available on the project website.

</details>


### [10] [ModTrack: Sensor-Agnostic Multi-View Tracking via Identity-Informed PHD Filtering with Covariance Propagation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.15812)
*Aditya Iyer,Jack Roberts,Nora Ayanian*

Main category: cs.CV

TL;DR: ModTrack is a modular multi-view multi-object tracker that confines learning to perception, uses analytical uncertainty-aware fusion and GM-PHD-based tracking, matches end-to-end accuracy, and generalizes across sensors/modalities without retraining the core.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: End-to-end MV-MOT achieves high accuracy but lacks principled uncertainty handling and does not generalize across sensor layouts/modalities/datasets without retraining, making deployment inflexible—especially under viewpoint changes and occlusions that break identity consistency.

Method: Limit learning to Detection/Feature Extraction; convert each sensor’s output into calibrated position–covariance pairs (z, R). Perform cross-view clustering and precision-weighted fusion to obtain unified estimates (ẑ, R̂). Use a feedback-coupled, identity-informed GM-PHD filter with HMM motion modes for data association and temporal tracking, enabling robustness to missed detections and heavy occlusions. All fusion/association/tracking steps use closed-form analytical methods with traceable uncertainty.

Result: On WildTrack, ModTrack attains 95.5 IDF1 and 91.4 MOTA—over 21 points better than prior modular methods and competitive with SOTA end-to-end approaches. The same tracker core transfers to MultiviewX and RadarScenes by only swapping the perception module, demonstrating cross-dataset and cross-modality generalization.

Conclusion: A modular, uncertainty-calibrated pipeline can rival end-to-end MV-MOT performance while providing sensor-agnostic transfer and deployment flexibility; decoupling the learned perception from analytical fusion/tracking yields strong accuracy, traceable uncertainty, and easy adaptation to new sensors and domains.

Abstract: Multi-View Multi-Object Tracking (MV-MOT) aims to localize and maintain consistent identities of objects observed by multiple sensors. This task is challenging, as viewpoint changes and occlusion disrupt identity consistency across views and time. Recent end-to-end approaches address this by jointly learning 2D Bird's Eye View (BEV) representations and identity associations, achieving high tracking accuracy. However, these methods offer no principled uncertainty accounting and remain tightly coupled to their training configuration, limiting generalization across sensor layouts, modalities, or datasets without retraining. We propose ModTrack, a modular MV-MOT system that matches end-to-end performance while providing cross-modal, sensor-agnostic generalization and traceable uncertainty. ModTrack confines learning methods to just the \textit{Detection and Feature Extraction} stage of the MV-MOT pipeline, performing all fusion, association, and tracking with closed-form analytical methods. Our design reduces each sensor's output to calibrated position-covariance pairs $(\mathbf{z}, R)$; cross-view clustering and precision-weighted fusion then yield unified estimates $(\hat{\mathbf{z}}, \hat{R})$ for identity assignment and temporal tracking. A feedback-coupled, identity-informed Gaussian Mixture Probability Hypothesis Density (GM-PHD) filter with HMM motion modes uses these fused estimates to maintain identities under missed detections and heavy occlusion. ModTrack achieves 95.5 IDF1 and 91.4 MOTA on \textit{WildTrack}, surpassing all prior modular methods by over 21 points and rivaling the state-of-the-art end-to-end methods while providing deployment flexibility they cannot. Specifically, the same tracker core transfers unchanged to \textit{MultiviewX} and \textit{RadarScenes}, with only perception-module replacement required to extend to new domains and sensor modalities.

</details>


### [11] [Conflict-Aware Multimodal Fusion for Ambivalence and Hesitancy Recognition](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.15818)
*Salah Eddine Bekhouche,Hichem Telli,Azeddine Benlamoudi,Salah Eddine Herrouz,Abdelmalik Taleb-Ahmed,Abdenour Hadid*

Main category: cs.CV

TL;DR: ConflictAwareAH is a simple, efficient multimodal model that detects ambivalence/hesitancy by explicitly modeling cross‑modal disagreements (text–audio–video embedding differences), achieving state‑of‑the‑art Macro F1 on ABAW10 BAH and notably boosting the NoAH class.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Ambivalence/hesitancy cues often reside in inconsistencies across what is said, how it is said, and facial expressions. Text‑dominant systems over‑detect A/H and struggle to confirm its absence; a model must capture both conflict (A/H) and consistency (NoAH).

Method: Use three pretrained encoders for video, audio, and text. Compute pairwise element‑wise absolute differences between modality embeddings as conflict features; combine them with modality embeddings for classification. Add a text‑guided late‑fusion at inference by blending a text‑only auxiliary head with the full model. Training runs on a single GPU in <25 minutes.

Result: On ABAW10 BAH: 0.694 Macro F1 (labeled test) and 0.715 (private leaderboard), >10 points over published multimodal baselines. Improves F1‑NoAH by +4.6 vs text‑only and halves the class‑performance gap. Text‑guided late fusion adds +4.1 Macro F1.

Conclusion: Explicitly encoding cross‑modal disagreement/consistency is highly effective for A/H recognition, delivering strong, efficient performance and better balance across classes. Simple conflict features plus lightweight late fusion set a new benchmark on BAH and suggest conflict‑aware designs are promising for clinical affect analysis.

Abstract: Ambivalence and hesitancy (A/H) are subtle affective states where a person shows conflicting signals through different channels -- saying one thing while their face or voice tells another story. Recognising these states automatically is valuable in clinical settings, but it is hard for machines because the key evidence lives in the \emph{disagreements} between what is said, how it sounds, and what the face shows.
  We present \textbf{ConflictAwareAH}, a multimodal framework built for this problem. Three pre-trained encoders extract video, audio, and text representations. Pairwise conflict features -- element-wise absolute differences between modality embeddings -- serve as \emph{bidirectional} cues: large cross-modal differences flag A/H, while small differences confirm behavioural consistency and anchor the negative class. This conflict-aware design addresses a key limitation of text-dominant approaches, which tend to over-detect A/H (high F1-AH) while struggling to confirm its absence: our multimodal model improves F1-NoAH by +4.6 points over text alone and halves the class-performance gap. A complementary \emph{text-guided late fusion} strategy blends a text-only auxiliary head with the full model at inference, adding +4.1 Macro F1.
  On the BAH dataset from the ABAW10 Ambivalence/Hesitancy Challenge, our method reaches \textbf{0.694 Macro F1} on the labelled test split and \textbf{0.715} on the private leaderboard, outperforming published multimodal baselines by over 10 points -- all on a single GPU in under 25 minutes of training.

</details>

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Automated 3D CT report generators miss pathologies. The authors hypothesize the root cause is not the language model but a collapsed, low-rank visual embedding that cannot carry sufficient clinical signals, and that naive retrieval doesn’t help and can harm.

Method: 1) Empirically analyze contrastive 3D CT embeddings, revealing extreme dimensional concentration (≈2 effective dimensions out of 512). 2) Show scaling the language model yields no gains, implicating the visual encoder as the bottleneck. 3) Propose AdaRAG-CT: an adaptive augmentation framework that performs controlled retrieval of supplementary textual information and selectively integrates it during generation to compensate for missing visual signals.

Naive/static retrieval fails to help and can degrade performance; ablations indicate both retrieval and generation components are necessary for the gains. Code is released.

An adaptive, selectively integrated RAG pipeline effectively counteracts this bottleneck and sets a new SOTA on CT-RATE, highlighting the need to fix visual encodings and to use controlled, adaptive retrieval rather than naive approaches.

Abstract: Automated radiology report generation from 3D CT volumes often suffers from incomplete pathology coverage. We provide empirical evidence that this limitation stems from a representational bottleneck: contrastive 3D CT embeddings encode discriminative pathology signals, yet exhibit severe dimensional concentration, with as few as 2 effective dimensions out of 512. Corroborating this, scaling the language model yields no measurable improvement, suggesting that the bottleneck lies in the visual representation rather than the generator. This bottleneck limits both generation and retrieval; naive static retrieval fails to improve clinical efficacy and can even degrade performance. We propose \textbf{AdaRAG-CT}, an adaptive augmentation framework that compensates for this visual bottleneck by introducing supplementary textual information through controlled retrieval and selectively integrating it during generation. On the CT-RATE benchmark, AdaRAG-CT achieves state-of-the-art clinical efficacy, improving Clinical F1 from 0.420 (CT-Agent) to 0.480 (+6 points); ablation studies confirm that both the retrieval and generation components contribute to the improvement. Code is available at https://github.com/renjie-liang/Adaptive-RAG-for-3DCT-Report-Generation.

Abstract: We introduce FEEL (Force-Enhanced Egocentric Learning), the first large-scale dataset pairing force measurements gathered from custom piezoresistive gloves with egocentric video. Our gloves enable scalable data collection, and FEEL contains approximately 3 million force-synchronized frames of natural unscripted manipulation in kitchen environments, with 45% of frames involving hand-object contact. Because force is the underlying cause that drives physical interaction, it is a critical primitive for physical action understanding. We demonstrate the utility of force for physical action understanding through application of FEEL to two families of tasks: (1) contact understanding, where we jointly perform temporal contact segmentation and pixel-level contacted object segmentation; and, (2) action representation learning, where force prediction serves as a self-supervised pretraining objective for video backbones. We achieve state-of-the-art temporal contact segmentation results and competitive pixel-level segmentation results without any need for manual contacted object segmentation annotations. *Jakaria Rabbi,Nilanjan Ray,Dana Cobzas*

Main category: cs.CV

TL;DR: Two-stage approach that first discovers pseudo disease labels from SDF-based 3D shape embeddings, then disentangles aging vs. pathology in a variational latent space using age labels and a multi-objective loss, achieving near-supervised performance with improved reconstruction, control, and explainability on hippocampus and femur datasets.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Disease and normal aging both alter 3D anatomical shapes, but diagnosis labels are scarce and effects overlap, hindering interpretable biomarkers and patient stratification. The goal is to separate pathological from physiological shape changes without relying on extensive ground-truth labels.

Method: Stage 1: Train an implicit neural model with signed distance functions to learn stable latent shape embeddings; cluster the latent space to obtain pseudo disease labels without diagnosis supervision. Stage 2: Learn a compact variational latent space that disentangles factors using the discovered pseudo disease labels and known age labels; enforce separation and controllability with a multi-objective loss combining covariance penalization and supervised contrastive terms; support high-fidelity reconstruction and controllable synthesis.

Abstract: Disentangling pathological changes from physiological aging in 3D medical shapes is crucial for developing interpretable biomarkers and patient stratification. However, this separation is challenging when diagnosis labels are limited or unavailable, since disease and aging often produce overlapping effects on shape changes, obscuring clinically relevant shape patterns. To address this challenge, we propose a two-stage framework combining unsupervised disease discovery with self-supervised disentanglement of implicit shape representations. In the first stage, we train an implicit neural model with signed distance functions to learn stable shape embeddings. We then apply clustering on the shape latent space, which yields pseudo disease labels without using ground-truth diagnosis during discovery. In the second stage, we disentangle factors in a compact variational space using pseudo disease labels discovered in the first stage and the ground truth age labels available for all subjects. We enforce separation and controllability with a multi-objective disentanglement loss combining covariance and a supervised contrastive loss. On ADNI hippocampus and OAI distal femur shapes, we achieve near-supervised performance, improving disentanglement and reconstruction over state-of-the-art unsupervised baselines, while enabling high-fidelity reconstruction, controllable synthesis, and factor-based explainability. ### [16] [Sparse but not Simpler: A Multi-Level Interpretability Analysis of Vision Transformers](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.15919)
*Siyu Zhang*

Main category: cs.CV

TL;DR: Pruning Vision Transformers creates sparser circuits (≈2.5× fewer edges) but does not make them more semantically interpretable across neurons, features, circuits, or attributions; sparsity mostly redistributes computation instead of isolating simpler modules.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: The common belief that sparse networks are inherently more interpretable lacks systematic validation in vision models. Prior evidence from language models (compact circuits) motivates testing whether structural sparsity itself yields semantic interpretability gains, and developing a comprehensive evaluation framework to assess this claim.

Method: Use DeiT-III B/16 Vision Transformers pruned with Wanda. Introduce IMPACT, a multi-level interpretability evaluation spanning (1) neuron selectivity, (2) layer representations via BatchTopK sparse autoencoders (SAEs), (3) task circuits via learnable node masking, and (4) model-level attribution using transformer attribution evaluated with insertion/deletion metrics.

Result: Sparsity yields a clear structural change—extracted circuits have ~2.5× fewer edges—yet the fraction of active nodes is similar or higher than in dense models, indicating computation is redistributed rather than simplified. No consistent improvements are observed in neuron-level selectivity, SAE feature interpretability, or attribution faithfulness.

Conclusion: Structural weight sparsity alone does not reliably improve interpretability in Vision Transformers. Compactness of circuits is an insufficient proxy; broader, multi-level evaluation is necessary, and interpretability likely requires targeted or interpretability-driven sparsification/training rather than generic pruning.

Abstract: Sparse neural networks are often hypothesized to be more interpretable than dense models, motivated by findings that weight sparsity can produce compact circuits in language models. However, it remains unclear whether structural sparsity itself leads to improved semantic interpretability. In this work, we systematically evaluate the relationship between weight sparsity and interpretability in Vision Transformers using DeiT-III B/16 models pruned with Wanda. To assess interpretability comprehensively, we introduce \textbf{IMPACT}, a multi-level framework that evaluates interpretability across four complementary levels: neurons, layer representations, task circuits, and model-level attribution. Layer representations are analyzed using BatchTopK sparse autoencoders, circuits are extracted via learnable node masking, and explanations are evaluated with transformer attribution using insertion and deletion metrics. Our results reveal a clear structural effect but limited interpretability gains. Sparse models produce circuits with approximately $2.5\times$ fewer edges than dense models, yet the fraction of active nodes remains similar or higher, indicating that pruning redistributes computation rather than isolating simpler functional modules. Consistent with this observation, sparse models show no systematic improvements in neuron-level selectivity, SAE feature interpretability, or attribution faithfulness. These findings suggest that structural sparsity alone does not reliably yield more interpretable vision models, highlighting the importance of evaluation frameworks that assess interpretability beyond circuit compactness.

### [21] [Mostly Text, Smart Visuals: Asymmetric Text-Visual Pruning for Large Vision-Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.16001)
*Sijie Li,Biao Qian,Jungong Han*

Main category: cs.CV

TL;DR: ATV-Pruning is a modality-aware pruning method for LVLMs that calibrates with all text tokens and a selectively chosen subset of visual tokens, reflecting higher text sensitivity and higher visual redundancy, achieving state-of-the-art accuracy–sparsity trade-offs.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Prior LVLM pruning treats visual and textual modalities uniformly, ignoring their divergent sensitivities. This can misestimate importance and harm accuracy. The authors aim to exploit modality-specific behavior to prune more aggressively (especially visually) while preserving performance.

Method: They decouple text and vision pathways to analyze pruning sensitivity, finding text more sensitive and vision more redundant (up to ~50% sparsity). They propose ATV-Pruning: (1) build a calibration pool using all textual tokens plus an adaptively selected subset of visual tokens; (2) employ a layer-adaptive selection strategy to identify informative visual tokens; (3) compute an importance metric (weights+activations) using this asymmetric calibration to guide weight pruning.

Result: Across standard multimodal benchmarks, ATV-Pruning outperforms prior pruning baselines, maintaining better accuracy at comparable or higher sparsity, particularly by exploiting higher visual redundancy. The abstract does not provide exact numbers but claims consistent gains and feasibility of high visual sparsity (~50%).

Conclusion: Modality-aware, asymmetric calibration is crucial for accurate LVLM pruning. Using all text tokens and layer-adaptively chosen visual tokens yields a stronger importance estimate, enabling greater sparsity—especially in visual pathways—without sacrificing performance.

Abstract: Network pruning is an effective technique for enabling lightweight Large Vision-Language Models (LVLMs), which primarily incorporates both weights and activations into the importance metric. However, existing efforts typically process calibration data from different modalities in a unified manner, overlooking modality-specific behaviors. This raises a critical challenge: how to address the divergent behaviors of textual and visual tokens for accurate pruning of LVLMs. To this end, we systematically investigate the sensitivity of visual and textual tokens to the pruning operation by decoupling their corresponding weights, revealing that: (i) the textual pathway should be calibrated via text tokens, since it exhibits higher sensitivity than the visual pathway; (ii) the visual pathway exhibits high redundancy, permitting even 50% sparsity. Motivated by these insights, we propose a simple yet effective Asymmetric Text-Visual Weight Pruning method for LVLMs, dubbed ATV-Pruning, which establishes the importance metric for accurate weight pruning by selecting the informative tokens from both textual and visual pathways. Specifically, ATV-Pruning integrates two primary innovations: first, a calibration pool is adaptively constructed by drawing on all textual tokens and a subset of visual tokens; second, we devise a layer-adaptive selection strategy to yield important visual tokens. Finally, extensive experiments across standard multimodal benchmarks verify the superiority of our ATV-Pruning over state-of-the-art methods.

TL;DR: FlatLands introduces a large dataset and benchmark for predicting full bird’s-eye-view floor traversability maps from a single egocentric RGB image, evaluates a spectrum of modeling paradigms, and provides an end-to-end RGB-to-floormap pipeline to standardize and advance uncertainty-aware indoor mapping for navigation.

Motivation: Single first-person images see only a small portion of the floor, whereas indoor navigation needs complete, metric, traversable floor maps; the community lacks a unified dataset/benchmark to study single-view BEV floor completion and its uncertainties, including generalization beyond training distributions.

Method: Curate FlatLands: 270,575 observations from 17,656 real indoor scenes across six datasets with aligned observation, visibility, validity, and ground-truth BEV maps. Define in-distribution and out-of-distribution evaluation protocols. Compare training-free methods, deterministic models, ensembles, and stochastic generative models, and instantiate an end-to-end monocular RGB-to-floormap pipeline.

Result: Provide a large, standardized dataset with alignment metadata; establish benchmark protocols; report empirical comparisons across multiple approach families; demonstrate a practical end-to-end pipeline for single-view BEV floor completion. Results highlight feasibility and enable uncertainty-aware evaluation, though specific numbers are not stated in the abstract.

Conclusion: FlatLands offers a rigorous testbed and baselines for generative, uncertainty-aware BEV floor completion from monocular images, facilitating research and development for embodied indoor navigation, including assessment of in- and out-of-distribution generalization.

Abstract: A single egocentric image typically captures only a small portion of the floor, yet a complete metric traversability map of the surroundings would better serve applications such as indoor navigation. We introduce FlatLands, a dataset and benchmark for single-view bird's-eye view (BEV) floor completion. The dataset contains 270,575 observations from 17,656 real metric indoor scenes drawn from six existing datasets, with aligned observation, visibility, validity, and ground-truth BEV maps, and the benchmark includes both in- and out-of-distribution evaluation protocols. We compare training-free approaches, deterministic models, ensembles, and stochastic generative models. Finally, we instantiate the task as an end-to-end monocular RGB-to-floormaps pipeline. FlatLands provides a rigorous testbed for uncertainty-aware indoor mapping and generative completion for embodied navigation.

Existing linear attention for ViTs often needs costly training from scratch, and decoder-oriented linearizations from LLMs transfer poorly. The goal is to reuse powerful pretrained VFMs while achieving linear-time attention.

Method: A staged adaptation: (1) Attention alignment—train vanilla linear attention in each block to approximate the original softmax attention. (3) Supervised fine-tuning—adapt the model to downstream tasks with labeled data.

Existing linear attention approaches for ViTs are typically trained from scratch, requiring substantial computational resources, while linearization-based methods developed for large language model decoders do not transfer well to ViTs. To address these challenges, we propose ViT-AdaLA, a novel framework for effectively adapting and transferring prior knowledge from VFMs to linear attention ViTs. ViT-AdaLA consists of three stages: attention alignment, feature alignment, and supervised fine-tuning. In the attention alignment stage, we align vanilla linear attention with the original softmax-based attention in each block to approximate the behavior of softmax attention. However, residual approximation errors inevitably accumulate across layers. We mitigate this by fine-tuning the linearized ViT to align its final-layer features with a frozen softmax VFM teacher. Finally, the adapted prior knowledge is transferred to downstream tasks through supervised fine-tuning. Extensive experiments on classification and segmentation tasks demonstrate the effectiveness and generality of ViT-AdaLA over various state-of-the-art linear attention counterpart.

### [25] [Attribution Upsampling should Redistribute, Not Interpolate](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.16067)
*Vincenzo Buono,Peyman Sheikholharam Mashhadi,Mahmoud Rahat,Prayag Tiwari,Stefan Byttner*

Main category: cs.CV

TL;DR: Standard image interpolation corrupts saliency/attribution maps. The paper reframes upsampling as semantic, mass-redistribution and proposes Universal Semantic-Aware Upsampling (USU), an axiomatic, ratio-form operator that preserves attribution mass and rankings, yielding more faithful, coherent explanations across datasets.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Attribution maps are often produced at low resolution and upsampled with bilinear/bicubic methods built for natural images. These introduce aliasing, ringing, and boundary bleeding, creating spurious high-importance regions that misrepresent model reasoning. A principled, semantics-aware upsampling that preserves attribution properties is needed.

Method: Define upsampling as a mass-redistribution problem governed by model-derived semantic boundaries rather than as signal interpolation. Extend the axiomatic framework of feature attribution to upsampling via four desiderata. Prove that conventional interpolation violates three axioms, that these three force any valid operator into a ratio-form redistribution, and that the fourth uniquely selects a potential within this family—yielding USU. USU provably preserves attribution mass and relative importance ordering and is presented as a universal drop-in for attribution methods.

Result: Formal proofs establish mass preservation and ranking invariance. Controlled experiments with models having known attribution priors validate the guarantees. Benchmarks on ImageNet, CIFAR-10, and CUB-200 show consistent improvements in attribution faithfulness and qualitatively more semantically aligned saliency maps compared with bilinear/bicubic interpolation.

Conclusion: Treating saliency upsampling as semantic mass redistribution, not interpolation, avoids artifacts and yields faithful explanations. USU provides the uniquely axiomatic, semantics-aware operator that preserves attribution structure and improves both quantitative faithfulness and qualitative coherence across tasks.

Abstract: Attribution methods in explainable AI rely on upsampling techniques that were designed for natural images, not saliency maps. Standard bilinear and bicubic interpolation systematically corrupts attribution signals through aliasing, ringing, and boundary bleeding, producing spurious high-importance regions that misrepresent model reasoning. We identify that the core issue is treating attribution upsampling as an interpolation problem that operates in isolation from the model's reasoning, rather than a mass redistribution problem where model-derived semantic boundaries must govern how importance flows. We present Universal Semantic-Aware Upsampling (USU), a principled method that reformulates upsampling through ratio-form mass redistribution operators, provably preserving attribution mass and relative importance ordering. Extending the axiomatic tradition of feature attribution to upsampling, we formalize four desiderata for faithful upsampling and prove that interpolation structurally violates three of them. These same three force any redistribution operator into a ratio form; the fourth selects the unique potential within this family, yielding USU. Controlled experiments on models with known attribution priors verify USU's formal guarantees; evaluation across ImageNet, CIFAR-10, and CUB-200 confirms consistent faithfulness improvements and qualitatively superior, semantically coherent explanations.

In the motivating application to placenta MRI, our formulation jointly reconstructs individual placentas, aligns them to a population-derived implicit template, and enables voxel-wise intensity mapping in a unified canonical space. Based on the 3D guidance scene, the primary object is anchored through precise global-to-local geometric alignment (registration), while subsequent geometries are integrated using a differentiable Signed Distance Field (SDF)-based optimization that explicitly penalizes geometry intersections. ### [29] [Parallel In-context Learning for Large Vision Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.16092)
*Shin'ya Yamaguchi,Daiki Chijiwa,Tamao Sakao,Taku Hasegawa*

Main category: cs.CV

TL;DR: Parallel-ICL splits long multimodal demonstration contexts into parallel, shorter chunks and aggregates their per-chunk logits with a weighted Product-of-Experts, yielding near full-context accuracy with much lower latency on VQA, captioning, and classification.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: MM-ICL performance improves with more demonstrations, but Transformer attention scales quadratically with context length, causing large inference latency in LVLMs. There is a need to preserve accuracy benefits of long contexts while reducing compute and wall-clock time.

Method: A plug-and-play inference scheme: (1) partition the demonstration set into multiple shorter chunks using clustering to maximize inter-chunk diversity; (2) run the LVLM on each chunk independently and in parallel; (3) compute per-chunk logits; (4) weight chunks by query–chunk similarity; (5) combine via a weighted Product-of-Experts at the logit level to approximate full-context predictions. No retraining required.

Result: Across VQA, image captioning, and classification benchmarks, Parallel-ICL matches or closely approaches full-context MM-ICL accuracy while substantially reducing inference time, demonstrating favorable accuracy–efficiency trade-offs.

Conclusion: Parallel-ICL is an effective, general, and scalable inference-time approach for MM-ICL that maintains near full-context performance with significantly reduced latency, enabling practical dynamic task adaptation for LVLMs.

Abstract: Large vision-language models (LVLMs) employ multi-modal in-context learning (MM-ICL) to adapt to new tasks by leveraging demonstration examples. While increasing the number of demonstrations boosts performance, they incur significant inference latency due to the quadratic computational cost of Transformer attention with respect to the context length. To address this trade-off, we propose Parallel In-Context Learning (Parallel-ICL), a plug-and-play inference algorithm. Parallel-ICL partitions the long demonstration context into multiple shorter, manageable chunks. It processes these chunks in parallel and integrates their predictions at the logit level, using a weighted Product-of-Experts (PoE) ensemble to approximate the full-context output. Guided by ensemble learning theory, we introduce principled strategies for Parallel-ICL: (i) clustering-based context chunking to maximize inter-chunk diversity and (ii) similarity-based context compilation to weight predictions by query relevance. Extensive experiments on VQA, image captioning, and classification benchmarks demonstrate that Parallel-ICL achieves performance comparable to full-context MM-ICL, while significantly improving inference speed. Our work offers an effective solution to the accuracy-efficiency trade-off in MM-ICL, enabling dynamic task adaptation with substantially reduced inference overhead.

  <summary>Details</summary>

This paper seeks to test and clarify that claim to guide model choice and method design.

Method: (1) Revisit the theoretical argument asserting unconstrained degrees of freedom that distort image–image distances under contrastive learning. (2) Reevaluate empirical misalignment indicators, comparing language–image models (CLIP, SigLIP) to image–image models (DINO, SigLIP2). (3) Run intra-modal benchmarks—image retrieval and few-shot classification—while explicitly addressing task ambiguity to see what actually drives performance.

Result: They show the posited degrees of freedom for image-distance distortion do not exist under the considered objectives. Empirical indicators behave similarly for language–image and image–image trained models, implying the observed effects are not specific to CLIP-like training. On retrieval and few-shot classification, gains come from mitigating task ambiguity rather than enforcing additional intra-modal alignment.

Conclusion: The intra-modal misalignment hypothesis for CLIP-like embeddings is unsupported. CLIP-style representations are not inherently ill-calibrated for image-only tasks; focusing on task ambiguity and evaluation design yields better outcomes than adding extra intra-modal constraints.

Abstract: Recent research suggested that the embeddings produced by CLIP-like contrastive language-image training are suboptimal for image-only tasks. The main theory is that the inter-modal (language-image) alignment loss ignores intra-modal (image-image) alignment, leading to poorly calibrated distances between images. In this study, we question this intra-modal misalignment hypothesis. We reexamine its foundational theoretical argument, the indicators used to support it, and the performance metrics affected. For the theoretical argument, we demonstrate that there are no such supposed degrees of freedom for image embedding distances. For the empirical measures, our findings reveal they yield similar results for language-image trained models (CLIP, SigLIP) and image-image trained models (DINO, SigLIP2). This indicates the observed phenomena do not stem from a misalignment specific to the former. Experiments on the commonly studied intra-modal tasks retrieval and few-shot classification confirm that addressing task ambiguity, not supposed misalignment, is key for best results.

### [33] [NanoGS: Training-Free Gaussian Splat Simplification](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.16103)
*Butian Xiong,Rong Liu,Tiantian Zhou,Meida Chen,Zhiwen Fan,Andrew Feng*

Main category: cs.CV

TL;DR: NanoGS is a training-free, CPU-efficient method that simplifies 3D Gaussian Splat (3DGS) scenes by locally merging splats, sharply reducing primitive counts while preserving rendering quality and full compatibility with standard 3DGS pipelines.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: 3DGS often needs millions of anisotropic splats, creating large storage/transmission burdens. Existing compression typically requires GPU-heavy, image-supervised post-training optimization with calibrated views, hindering practical deployment.

Method: Formulate simplification as local pairwise merging on a sparse spatial graph. Approximate each candidate pair with a single Gaussian using mass-preserved moment matching, and assess merge quality via a principled merge-cost comparing the original two-splat mixture to its one-splat approximation. Restrict candidates to local neighborhoods and select compatible pairs efficiently. Operates directly on pre-trained 3DGS, preserves standard parameterization, and runs on CPU.

Result: Across experiments, NanoGS markedly lowers the number of Gaussian primitives while maintaining high-fidelity novel view synthesis. It demonstrates efficient CPU execution and seamless drop-in use with existing renderers.

Conclusion: A practical, training-free simplification pipeline for 3DGS that delivers compact scene representations without sacrificing visual quality, enabling easier storage/transmission and immediate deployment in current rendering workflows.

Abstract: 3D Gaussian Splat (3DGS) enables high-fidelity, real-time novel view synthesis by representing scenes with large sets of anisotropic primitives, but often requires millions of Splats, incurring significant storage and transmission costs. Most existing compression methods rely on GPU-intensive post-training optimization with calibrated images, limiting practical deployment. We introduce NanoGS, a training-free and lightweight framework for Gaussian Splat simplification. Instead of relying on image-based rendering supervision, NanoGS formulates simplification as local pairwise merging over a sparse spatial graph. The method approximates a pair of Gaussians with a single primitive using mass preserved moment matching and evaluates merge quality through a principled merge cost between the original mixture and its approximation. By restricting merge candidates to local neighborhoods and selecting compatible pairs efficiently, NanoGS produces compact Gaussian representations while preserving scene structure and appearance. NanoGS operates directly on existing Gaussian Splat models, runs efficiently on CPU, and preserves the standard 3DGS parameterization, enabling seamless integration with existing rendering pipelines. Experiments demonstrate that NanoGS substantially reduces primitive count while maintaining high rendering fidelity, providing an efficient and practical solution for Gaussian Splat simplification. Our project website is available at https://saliteta.github.io/NanoGS/.

  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Clinical deployment of pathology VLMs is hindered by the lack of reliable automated evaluation that can catch subtle failures (e.g., hallucinations) and work on private datasets without references.

Method: Introduce PathGLS with three axes: Grounding (fine-grained visual–text alignment), Logic (NLI-based entailment graph consistency), and Stability (variance under adversarial visual–semantic perturbations). Supports patch- and whole-slide levels, aggregated into a comprehensive trust score.

Result: Across Quilt-1M, TCGA, REG2025, PathMMU, and TCGA-Sarcoma, PathGLS shows superior sensitivity to hallucinations (e.g., 40.2% sensitivity drop for hallucinated reports vs 2.1% for BERTScore) and correlates strongly with expert clinical error hierarchies (Spearman ρ=0.71, p<1e-4), outperforming LLM-based evaluators (e.g., Gemini 3.0 Pro ρ=0.39).

Conclusion: PathGLS is a robust reference-free evaluator that quantifies hallucination rates and robustness to domain shift, offering a practical criterion for benchmarking VLMs on private clinical datasets and guiding safer deployment.

Abstract: Vision-Language Models (VLMs) offer significant potential in computational pathology by enabling interpretable image analysis, automated reporting, and scalable decision support. However, their widespread clinical adoption remains limited due to the absence of reliable, automated evaluation metrics capable of identifying subtle failures such as hallucinations. To address this gap, we propose PathGLS, a novel reference-free evaluation framework that assesses pathology VLMs across three dimensions: Grounding (fine-grained visual-text alignment), Logic (entailment graph consistency using Natural Language Inference), and Stability (output variance under adversarial visual-semantic perturbations). PathGLS supports both patch-level and whole-slide image (WSI)-level analysis, yielding a comprehensive trust score. Experiments on Quilt-1M, TCGA, REG2025, PathMMU and TCGA-Sarcoma datasets demonstrate the superiority of PathGLS. Specifically, on the Quilt-1M dataset, PathGLS reveals a steep sensitivity drop of 40.2% for hallucinated reports compared to only 2.1% for BERTScore. Moreover, validation against expert-defined clinical error hierarchies reveals that PathGLS achieves a strong Spearman's rank correlation of $ρ=0.71$ ($p < 0.0001$), significantly outperforming Large Language Model (LLM)-based approaches (Gemini 3.0 Pro: $ρ=0.39$, $p < 0.0001$). These results establish PathGLS as a robust reference-free metric. By directly quantifying hallucination rates and domain shift robustness, it serves as a reliable criterion for benchmarking VLMs on private clinical datasets and informing safe deployment. Code can be found at: https://github.com/My13ad/PathGLS

### [38] [DualPrim: Compact 3D Reconstruction with Positive and Negative Primitives](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.16133)
*Xiaoxu Meng,Zhongmin Chen,Bo Yang,Weikai Chen,Weixiao Liu,Lin Gao*

Main category: cs.CV

  <summary>Details</summary>

Method: Represent each shape with positive superquadrics that build up volume and negative superquadrics that carve it away via a differentiable operator (closed-form boolean difference). Train end-to-end from multi-view images inside a volumetric differentiable renderer, and export meshes directly from the analytic boolean formulation.

Result: Empirically achieves state-of-the-art reconstruction accuracy while producing compact, structured, and interpretable outputs that outperform additive-only baselines in downstream usability.

Abstract: Neural reconstructions often trade structure for fidelity, yielding dense and unstructured meshes with irregular topology and weak part boundaries that hinder editing, animation, and downstream asset reuse. We present DualPrim, a compact and structured 3D reconstruction framework. Unlike additive-only implicit or primitive methods, DualPrim represents shapes with positive and negative superquadrics: the former builds the bases while the latter carves local volumes through a differentiable operator, enabling topology-aware modeling of holes and concavities. This additive-subtractive design increases the representational power without sacrificing compactness or differentiability. We embed DualPrim in a volumetric differentiable renderer, enabling end-to-end learning from multi-view images and seamless mesh export via closed-form boolean difference. Empirically, DualPrim delivers state-of-the-art accuracy and produces compact, structured, and interpretable outputs that better satisfy downstream needs than additive-only alternatives.

### [39] [When Generative Augmentation Hurts: A Benchmark Study of GAN and Diffusion Models for Bias Correction in AI Classification Systems](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.16134)
*Shesh Narayan Gupta,Nik Bear Brown*

Main category: cs.CV

TL;DR: GAN-based augmentation can hurt under extreme low-data imbalance: FastGAN worsened bias (likely via mode collapse), while Stable Diffusion 1.5 fine-tuned with LoRA achieved the best macro F1 and reduced bias; the harmful regime for GANs appears below ~20–50 images per class.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Understand when and why generative data augmentation helps or harms imbalanced classification, especially in low-sample regimes where practitioners often turn to generators but their failure modes are unclear.

Method: Controlled benchmark on Oxford-IIIT Pet with eight breeds made severe minorities. Compared three augmentation strategies: (1) traditional image transforms, (2) FastGAN, and (3) Stable Diffusion 1.5 fine-tuned with LoRA. Trained classifiers with each augmentation, evaluated macro F1 and bias gap across three random seeds, and inspected feature-space structure via t-SNE. All experiments ran on a 6–8 GB consumer GPU.

Result: FastGAN increased classifier bias: bias gap +20.7% with a large effect (Cohen’s d = +5.03, p = 0.013). t-SNE showed synthetic images for minority breeds forming tight, isolated clusters outside the real distribution—consistent with mode collapse. Stable Diffusion + LoRA yielded the best overall performance: highest macro F1 (0.9125 ± 0.0047) and a 13.1% reduction in bias gap vs the unaugmented baseline. Evidence suggests a sample-size boundary between ~20 and 50 images/class below which GAN augmentation becomes harmful in this setting.

Conclusion: Avoid GAN-based augmentation for extremely low-data minority classes; diffusion models with lightweight fine-tuning (LoRA) are safer and more effective here. There is likely a tipping point around 20–50 images/class for GAN utility, but broader studies are needed to generalize across domains. Findings are practically accessible (consumer GPU, no cloud).

Abstract: Generative models are widely used to compensate for class imbalance in AI training pipelines, yet their failure modes under low-data conditions are poorly understood. This paper reports a controlled benchmark comparing three augmentation strategies applied to a fine-grained animal classification task: traditional transforms, FastGAN, and Stable Diffusion 1.5 fine-tuned with Low-Rank Adaptation (LoRA). Using the Oxford-IIIT Pet Dataset with eight artificially underrepresented breeds, we find that FastGAN augmentation does not merely underperform at very low training set sizes but actively increases classifier bias, with a statistically significant large effect across three random seeds (bias gap increase: +20.7%, Cohen's d = +5.03, p = 0.013). The effect size here is large enough to give confidence in the direction of the finding despite the small number of seeds. Feature embedding analysis using t-distributed Stochastic Neighbor Embedding reveals that FastGAN images for severe-minority breeds form tight isolated clusters outside the real image distribution, a pattern consistent with mode collapse. Stable Diffusion with Low-Rank Adaptation produced the best results overall, achieving the highest macro F1 (0.9125 plus or minus 0.0047) and a 13.1% reduction in the bias gap relative to the unaugmented baseline. The data suggest a sample-size boundary somewhere between 20 and 50 training images per class below which GAN augmentation becomes harmful in this setting, though further work across additional domains is needed to establish where that boundary sits more precisely. All experiments run on a consumer-grade GPU with 6 to 8 GB of memory, with no cloud compute required.

### [41] [EFF-Grasp: Energy-Field Flow Matching for Physics-Aware Dexterous Grasp Generation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2603.16151)
*Yukun Zhao,Zichen Zhong,Yongshun Gong,Yilong Yin,Haoliang Sun*

Main category: cs.CV

TL;DR: EFF-Grasp replaces diffusion-based SDE grasp generation with a deterministic Flow-Matching ODE and adds training-free physics-aware energy guidance (via explicit energy functions and local Monte Carlo) to produce higher-quality, physically feasible dexterous grasps with far fewer sampling steps across five benchmarks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Diffusion/SDE grasp generators need many sequential denoising steps and can suffer from unstable trajectories that yield physically infeasible grasps. The goal is to make generation both more efficient (fewer steps) and more reliable (physics-consistent).

Method: Reformulate grasp synthesis as a deterministic ODE using Flow Matching to obtain smooth probability flows for stable generation. Enforce physical feasibility via a training-free guidance term: define an energy-guided target distribution with explicit physics energy functions encoding grasp constraints (e.g., penetration, force/torque limits, joint limits), and approximate the guidance during inference with a local Monte Carlo estimator that steers trajectories toward low-energy (feasible) regions.

Result: On five benchmark datasets, EFF-Grasp outperforms diffusion-based baselines in grasp quality and physical feasibility while requiring substantially fewer sampling steps.

Conclusion: Coupling Flow-Matching ODE sampling with training-free physics-energy guidance yields a stable, efficient, and physically aware dexterous grasp generator that avoids extra physics-based training or simulator feedback and improves both feasibility and quality over diffusion baselines.

Abstract: Denoising generative models have recently become the dominant paradigm for dexterous grasp generation, owing to their ability to model complex grasp distributions from large-scale data. However, existing diffusion-based methods typically formulate generation as a stochastic differential equation (SDE), which often requires many sequential denoising steps and introduces trajectory instability that can lead to physically infeasible grasps. In this paper, we propose EFF-Grasp, a novel Flow-Matching-based framework for physics-aware dexterous grasp generation. Specifically, we reformulate grasp synthesis as a deterministic ordinary differential equation (ODE) process, which enables efficient and stable generation through smooth probability flows. To further enforce physical feasibility, we introduce a training-free physics-aware energy guidance strategy. Our method defines an energy-guided target distribution using adapted explicit physical energy functions that capture key grasp constraints, and estimates the corresponding guidance term via a local Monte Carlo approximation during inference. In this way, EFF-Grasp dynamically steers the generation trajectory toward physically feasible regions without requiring additional physics-based training or simulation feedback. Extensive experiments on five benchmark datasets show that EFF-Grasp achieves superior performance in grasp quality and physical feasibility, while requiring substantially fewer sampling steps than diffusion-based baselines.
